# Chapter 6: Follow-up Care After AKI or AKD

Robust epidemiological data have revealed that people with CKD are at increased risk
of AKI, and those who survive AKI and/or AKD face heightened long-term risks of CKD,
cardiovascular events, recurrent AKI, and premature mortality, confirming a bidirectional
relationship between AKI, AKD and CKD. Emerging evidence suggests that systematic
approaches to post-AKI care, including nephrology follow-up, medication optimization, and
structured surveillance protocols, may mitigate some of these adverse outcomes. This chapter
T
synthesizes recent advances in management post-AKI/AKD and follow-up care to improve
long-term outcomes.
6.1 Planning follow-up care with people afteFr AKI and/or AKD
Recommendation 6.1.1: We recommend coordinated follow-up for children and adults
after AKI and/or AKD, with personalized care according to individual clinical context
and vulnerability risks (1B).
A
This recommendation places a high value on the potential benefits of individualized risk
assessment to deliver personalized care for children and adults following an episode of
illness affected by AKI and/or AKD. There is consistent evidence that AKD and all stages of
AKI are associatedR with adverse long-term outcomes. The recommendation recognizes that
people affected by AKI and/or AKD are heterogenous with varying needs and priorities. As a
clinical syndrome, AKI offers an opportunity to improve systems of care and outcomes for
people living with complex health needs. To achieve this, the recommendation encourages
healthcare professionals, patients (and carers), researchers, and policy makers to consider
D
implementing strategies that maximize the clinical utility of AKI as a marker of systemic
vulnerability and adverse outcomes, while minimizing the potential for treatment and
workload burden. There is insufficient evidence favoring one provider type or model of care
over any other; consequently, individual patient, healthcare professional, and local system
factors, including availability and expertise, are likely the best drivers of these decisions.
Key information
Balance of benefits and harms.
There is extensive evidence that AKI/AKD is associated with poor outcomes.59, 61, 1017,
1146-1154 Relevant health outcomes for children and adults with AKI and/or AKD go beyond
the initial episode of acute illness with a need to plan timely and tailored follow-up care to
reduce the risks, in the short, medium, and long-term, of adverse kidney and non-kidney
outcomes (Table 57). Reducing the risk of all-cause hospital readmission and mortality are
immediate outcomes for consideration.1155, 1156 Longer term adverse outcomes include de
novo CKD, CKD progression, kidney failure, and further episodes of AKI,1150, 1157 as well as
development of hypertension and cardiovascular disease (CVD). Physical, psychological, and
social support are also central to improving functional status and QoL.1158-1161 In addition, a
systematic review that compared the out-of-pocket payments for various conditions identified
319

kidney disease, including AKI. as the leading cause of catastrophic health expenditure across
lower-resource settings.742
T
F

322

Acute management of AKI and/or AKD without coordinated planning of timely
follow-up care has the potential to exacerbate patient harm. Risks in the short-term include
the potential for unplanned hospital readmission due to inadequate early post-AKI clinical
assessment (including BP and fluid status to ensure euvolemia) following suspension of
diuretics and other cardiovascular medication during an episode of acute illness;1162 failure to
ensure ongoing monitoring and management for people without resolution of AKI/AKD; and
inadequate follow-up to address underlying causes and vulnerabilities (e.g., frailty, social
needs including limited access to housing, food, transportation, and financial stability).1159,
1161 In the longer-term, potential harms might stem from inadequate evaluation for CKD and
T
ongoing assessment to determine the risks and benefits of initiating or restarting drugs of
cardiovascular prognostic benefit, particularly in adults.1163-1166 Without a comprehensive
approach that entails patient engagement, which places AKI in the context of supporting a
person’s physical and mental health to enable recovery in social functioning, there remains a
F
risk that post-AKI care may result in unnecessary treatment burden and duplication of clinical
workload.1158, 1159, 1166-1170
Potential psychosocial harms from clinical reassessment following AKI have been
A
reported by patients and include the treatment burden of post-hospitalization care resulting
from additional clinic visits, strain on patients and caregivers, and financial pressure because
of medical expenses.1169 Patients have described anxiety from competing health demands as
challenges.1168 Approaches that alleviate treatment burden while implementing a
R
comprehensive model of care may be more acceptable.
The systematic review for this guideline identified that interventions to implement
comprehensive post-AKI care were associated with reduced mortality (1 RCT and 3
obsDervational studies; RR: 0.75; 95% CI: 0.70–0.81) and a reduced risk of CKD requiring
maintenance dialysis (one observational study, HR: 0.58; 95% CI: 0.54–0.62), with uncertain
effects on other outcomes (Supplementary Table S92).1171-1174 In an RCT, 98 participants
with AKI were randomized to comprehensive multidisciplinary nephrology care (5 visits)
versus standard care for 12 months post-discharge.1173 Nephrologists were the principal
physicians providing coordination with other healthcare professionals to manage all transition
care post-AKI. This trial reported better BP control and lower UACR in the nephrology care
group, but there was no difference in mean eGFR or other kidney outcomes between the 2
groups at one year.
Several observational studies have highlighted gaps in follow-up care processes for
children and adults affected by AKI/AKD, reflecting missed opportunities across healthcare
systems.1175, 1176 However, there remains limited trial-based evidence on models to enhance
implementation of these recommended actions. Meta-analysis combining 5 observational
studies and 1 RCT in adults found that nephrology follow-up was associated with a lower risk
of death (RR: 0.84; 95% CI: 0.76–0.93) and possibly lower risk of recurrent AKI (RR: 0.57;
95% CI: 0.33–1.01; Supplementary Table S93).1177-1179 In particular, one study reported that
among adults who had received temporary dialysis during an admission with AKI, early
nephrology follow-up was associated with lower mortality.1180 However, the observational
323

studies included in this review consistently found that adults who had nephrology follow-up
were more likely to ultimately receive maintenance dialysis (CKD G5D); the reason for this
is unclear, but may relate to unaccounted confounding with the likelihood that people with
more advanced kidney disease post-AKI may be more likely to be followed by a
nephrologist.1180
Children with a history of AKI are also at increased risk for long-term kidney
sequelae, including recurrent AKI, proteinuria, hypertension, and progression to CKD, yet
most do not receive structured nephrology follow-up.1176 In a systematic review
T
encompassing over 6700 children with cardiac disease undergoing cardiac surgery,
subclinical injury markers, such as proteinuria and tubular damage biomarkers were common
after AKI, even without overt CKD, but nephrology follow-up occurred in fewer than 5% of
cases, underscoring the need for standardized surveillance protocols that incorporate risk
F
stratification by AKI severity and cardiac complexity, with long-term monitoring that extends
beyond SCr alone.1153
Certainty of evidence
A
There is moderate certainty of evidence that comprehensive care post-AKI in adults is
associated with reduced risk of death and maintenance dialysis (with low certainty of
evidence), with uncertain effects on other kidney-related outcomes.1171-1174 The certainty of
evidence was downgraded for methodological quality (including mostly nonrandomized
R
trials) and for maintenance dialysis, sparseness of studies. The overall certainty of evidence
was graded as moderate based primarily on certainty regarding mortality.
There is low certainty of evidence that follow-up by a nephrologist, specifically, in
aduDlts post-AKI is associated with lower risk of death and possibly recurrent AKI; although,
with a higher incidence of maintenance dialysis reported among people seen by
nephrologists.1177-1179 The certainty of evidence was downgraded for methodological quality
of mostly observational studies.
No eligible studies were conducted in children. However, the Work Group felt that
the findings in the adult literature were applicable to pediatric populations and did not rate
the findings any lower for indirectness when applied to the pediatric population.
Values and preferences.
A systematic review of patient experiences has highlighted major challenges for
people following an episode of illness affected by AKI. Difficulties include navigating
fragmented systems of care after hospital discharge; reduced well-being with limited ability
to carry out daily activities; uncertainties surrounding resolution of AKI; dealing with
competing health priorities; and unsustainable treatment burden associated with ongoing
monitoring.1181, 1182 People often lack awareness of their AKI diagnosis.1169, 1183-1185
Supporting people during this period of vulnerability to gain confidence in self-management,
with a focus on understanding and optimizing kidney health protection strategies in the
324

context of addressing wider health and social needs, is a key element of follow-up care after
AKI and/or AKD.1169
Resource use and costs.
The systematic follow-up of AKI survivors is associated with increased resource use
entailing additional laboratory testing, a nephrology workforce as well as a range of
multidisciplinary healthcare professionals. This may lead to increased rates of
rehospitalization and referral through earlier detection of adverse events. These costs may be
offset by better longer-term outcomes for AKI survivors, although this has not yet been
T
proven in RCTs.
Considerations for implementation.
Barriers to follow-up care include under-recognition of AKI by healthcare
F
professionals, lack of knowledge about the systemic and life-course impact of AKI by the
healthcare team, insufficient patient and healthcare professional knowledge about AKI and its
attendant risk, challenges integrating AKI and kidney health into the context of competing
priorities of care for comorbidities, a lack of evidence for effective interventions to improve
A
outcomes after AKI, and limited timely access to nephrology care. The role and resourcing of
primary care as well as the use of virtual or point-of-care follow-up strategies focused on
those at highest risk of short-term and long-term adverse outcomes may more effectively
target AKI survivors who are most likely to benefit but requires further evaluation.
R
Implementation should consider the use of approaches that alleviate treatment burden for
patients while delivering comprehensive and integrated models of care that improve patient
outcomes.
RatDionale
AKI is a common, harmful and costly clinical syndrome. It is a marker of systemic
vulnerability, with evidence of adverse kidney and non-kidney outcomes across all stages of
AKI. Significant gaps have been identified in the post-discharge care of AKI survivors.1181
Prior research has demonstrated that several domains of care could be improved for follow-
up care of people with AKI/AKD.
CKD assessment and management following AKI often falls short of best practices,
with many adults not receiving statins or RASi, despite evidence they can improve kidney
and cardiovascular outcomes.1165, 1186-1188 Recognition and follow-up of AKI after discharge
is poor due to deficits in communication about AKI, lack of healthcare professional
recognition of people at risk of adverse sequelae from AKI after hospital discharge, and an
already high burden of care and complexity due to comorbidities. Adults describe
prioritization of conditions other than AKI, variability in comprehension of the significance
of AKI and kidney health, and anxiety from discharge planning and competing health
demands, while healthcare professionals have identified care coordination and
communication as key challenges to providing effective care.1169, 1181
325

Appropriate clinical oversight is necessary to support the development and effective
communication of a discharge plan that clarifies the purpose and timing of clinical follow-up
including the implementation of kidney health protection strategies (e.g., monitoring of
kidney health status and medicines optimization). Furthermore, people affected by AKI often
feel vulnerable and in the context of reduced patient and system capacity may benefit from
support coordinating timely multiprofessional follow-up.1169 Organizing early clinical follow-
up prior to discharge is necessary for those at high risk of hospital readmission. Clarity on the
purpose of follow-up at 3 months after discharge needs to be clearly communicated with
people affected by AKI/AKD and care professionals including those in primary care.
T
Based upon best available evidence, consensus guidance highlights key actions
required to improve AKI and/or AKD aftercare in both high- and low-resource settings. To
maximize clinical utility and minimize treatment burden, AKI and/or AKD need to be placed
F
in clinical and social context with coordinated follow-up care needing to take into account:
patient-specific risk factors; characteristics of the AKI episode; and the degree of resolution
of AKI/AKD. A holistic approach considers the interaction between emotional, cognitive,
and social vulnerabilities with the severity of the illness event and underlying comorbidities
A
(Figure 20).1159
R
D
Figure 20 | Strategies to deliver holistic follow-up care with children and adults after
AKI/AKD.1159 AKD, acute kidney disease; AKI, acute kidney injury
AKD affects a substantial proportion of children following AKI, with reported
incidence ranging from 17% to >50%, and consistently predicts adverse outcomes, including
CKD. Risk factors for AKD and its progression include higher AKI severity and duration,
326

persistent oliguria, anemia, glomerulonephritis, tubulointerstitial nephritis, and the need for
RRT. Critically ill children have a particularly high burden of AKI and AKD. Importantly,
AKD is predictive of MAKE and reduced eGFR at both 90 days and longer-term follow-up,
even among children without prior AKI.62, 1189-1193 This suggests that AKD is a clinically
relevant, high-risk condition that warrants structured post-discharge monitoring to mitigate
progression to CKD in children.
Special considerations
Adults with heart failure. Recognizing that a high proportion of readmissions relate to
T
people with pulmonary edema, post-AKI care needs to be aligned with and build on evidence
underpinning heart failure management.1155, 1156, 1194, 1195 In people with heart failure, caution
is necessary when interpreting kidney function tests (including changes in eGFR) as rises in
SCr are common and may reflect changes in hemodynamics (for instance, after initiation of
F
RASi or SGLT2i) rather than be due to intrinsic kidney injury.1196-1198 As such, placing
kidney function tests in clinical context is essential, with decongestion and assessment of
volume status to achieve “clinical euvolemia” being key elements of post-AKI care to
improve outcomes.1196
A
Practice Point 6.1.1: Transition of care from hospital to home requires clear
communication between inpatient and outpatient care teams, an identified healthcare
professional for AKI follow-up, and resources for patient engagement and education
R
about their kidney health and kidney health protection.
Incorporating specific kidney health protection plans into follow-up care pathways
requires communication between inpatient and outpatient healthcare professionals and
cooDrdinated planning with integration between healthcare professionals and clinical services.
This may involve several healthcare professionals and other clinical services, such as
postoperative care teams, comprehensive geriatric assessments for frail individuals, oncology,
or heart failure services, in addition to primary care.
The timing of follow-up should be individualized based on patient-specific risk
factors, comorbidities, the characteristics of the AKI episode (e.g., severity and duration), and
the extent of AKI/AKD resolution. Guidance for CKD evaluation at 3 months post-AKI
(Section 6.4) should be communicated and coordinated with the appropriate healthcare
professional prior to hospital discharge. Early, coordinated follow-up is particularly important
for people who: have a high risk of hospital readmission due to comorbid conditions (e.g.,
heart failure, frailty) or a significant acute illness (e.g., episode of temporary RRT, ICU
admission); require early clinical review to assess BP, volume status, or to safely reintroduce
or dose adjust medications withheld during hospitalization or introduce new kidney-
protective medications; need early evaluation of kidney function, especially in cases of
incomplete resolution of AKI where further diagnostic testing may be warranted, or where
kidney function remains dynamic at time of discharge; and/or for people experiencing social
vulnerability, which may impact their ability to access or adhere to follow-up care.
327

Early follow-up appointments are ideally scheduled prior to hospital discharge to
ensure timely post-acute care. Care coordinators and/or nurse navigators can play a key role
in supporting care transitions by promoting continuity, delivering kidney health education,
and minimizing treatment burden from multiple healthcare professionals, while ensuring that
follow-up care remains aligned with the person’ goals, values, and preferences.1199 This
includes facilitating coordinated follow-up for patients discharged to other institutions,
rehabilitation, or convalescent facilities.
There is also growing literature and expert consensus supporting the involvement of
T
pharmacists, both in the inpatient and outpatient setting and in collaboration with primary
care teams, to enhance medication management and optimize follow-up care after
AKI/AKD.1200
F
Practice Point 6.1.2: Provide discharge summaries for children and adults with AKI
that include details of the AKI episode, status of the resolution of AKI, medication
management, and follow-up plans with recommendations.
A
AKI is frequently under-recognized and poorly documented at discharge, especially in
children.1201, 1202 Studies have also shown that AKI is often not identified in primary care
records, leading to loss of the diagnosis during care transitions and limiting ongoing
management, including during future episodes of acute illness.1176, 1190, 1203 Most adults
R
affected by AKI are unclear on the diagnosis and its relevance. Caregivers for children may
also be unaware their child experienced AKI and targeted education is often lacking. In a
recent cross-sectional study, 75% of caregivers were unaware their child had experienced
AKI, and fewer than 40% recalled any discussion of kidney injury during the hospitalization.
NepDhrology consultation improved recognition, but substantial knowledge gaps persisted
across services.1204 For both children and adults, standardized discharge summaries should
include details of the AKI episode, status of the resolution of AKI, medication management
including reasons for medication changes and guidance regarding if, when, and how to
initiate or restart drugs, with a follow-up plan outlining ongoing monitoring of kidney health
status.
Given the high frequency of comorbid conditions and multidisciplinary outpatient
care, effective information-sharing is essential, not only with general practitioners and
pediatricians providing primary care, but also with relevant subspecialists such as cardiology,
transplant, or oncology, including specialist nurses. Embedding kidney health monitoring into
these care pathways, along with clear guidance on when to refer back to nephrology, is
expected to enhance patient experience and continuity of care.1176
To address these major gaps in follow-up care, multiprofessional models that entail
early and ongoing patient education, beginning during admission and reinforced at follow-up
visits in primary care have been developed, with findings to date demonstrating
improvements in care processes and patients’ knowledge of kidney health.1205-1207
Furthermore, audit and feedback interventions in conjunction with multiprofessional
328

education, leading to the development of systems-level improvement plans show promise and
warrant further evaluation.1202, 1206, 1208, 1209
Practice Point 6.1.3: A comprehensive clinical assessment of children and adults
following an episode of illness affected by AKI/AKD includes assessment of blood
pressure (BP) and volume status, medication reconciliation/review, and kidney function
and albuminuria after hospital discharge.
AKI survivors have a high rate of rehospitalization and significantly elevated risks of
T
major adverse cardiovascular and kidney events.207, 1146, 1160 Although interventional evidence
is currently low, a standardized approach to assessment may address observed care gaps in
AKI survivors. Informed by best available evidence, consensus work has provided guidance
regarding the timeliness and intensity of follow-up as well as “what” follow-up care after
F
AKI/AKD should entail. The Kidney function check, Advocacy, Medications, Pressure, Sick
day protocols (KAMPS) framework (Table 58) identifies key actions that support the health
and wellbeing of people who have had an AKI/AKD event.
A
Table 58 |.Post-AKI/AKD kidney health care framework1210
KAMPS Framework Components
Kidney function check Measure kidney function (e.g., serum creatinine or cystatin C), urinalysis,
assessment of albuminuria
Advocacy RPatient education about kidney function and AKI, what to expect in
resolution of AKI, and self-care strategies
Medications Medication review and reconciliation
Pressure Blood pressure evaluation and adherence to guideline-recommended targets
SicDk day protocols Patient information about how to self-manage medications in the event of
dehydration or acute illness
AKD, acute kidney disease; AKI, acute kidney injury
Comprehensive assessment of kidney function includes evaluation of eGFR (e.g.,
SCr, cystatin C) as well as albuminuria. BP control and volume assessment are particularly
important in the post-AKI period as antihypertensive medications and diuretics are frequently
stopped or modified during an AKI event.1211, 1212 A Canadian cohort study showed that heart
failure accounted for 13% of the primary hospital readmission diagnoses.1156, 1162, 1213, 1214 A
cohort study in Scotland found that 26.6% of rehospitalizations at 90-days related to acute
pulmonary edema.1155
Medication review, reconciliation, and adjustment, if necessary, can be provided by
many healthcare professionals, including pharmacists, and primary care physicians. The
original clinical indication for a drug should be reviewed. If a specific contraindication for a
drug has been identified, this should be documented and an alternative medication should be
considered.
329

Practice Point 6.1.4: Provide children and adults (and their caregivers) with a history of
AKI and/or AKD access to education about kidney health, sick day guidance and
kidney care, and psychosocial support to enhance functional status and quality of life.
Patients and caregivers often lack awareness of the long-term consequences of
AKI/AKD and may not recognize the importance of ongoing monitoring or early intervention
during intercurrent illness.1181 Children and adults who have sustained AKI and/or AKD are
at risk of recurrence. Educational efforts should include recognition of early warning signs,
appropriate fluid and medication management during illness, and guidance on when to seek
T
medical attention. Structured education addressing kidney health can empower patients and
families to actively participate in resolution of AKI and long-term self-care. Tailoring this
educational content to developmental stage, health literacy, and local context enhances
engagement and may reduce disparities in outcomes following AKI.
F
People require ongoing education about their kidney health including the rationale for
eGFR and UACR monitoring as well as best practices for kidney care including diet and
lifestyle recommendations.1207 Different online resources exist but it is important to tailor
A
them to the patient population and healthcare setting. An example is
https://www.thinkkidneys.nhs.uk/aki/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/2016/07/Infographic-poster-
March-2017.pdf
R
Support to optimize medication management (Section 7.3) includes avoidance of
NSAIDs and minimization of other nephrotoxic drugs, unless the benefits outweigh risks and
there is no alternative. Recognizing the association between AKI and frailty, interventions to
improve muscle mass/strength may be beneficial to enhance functional outcomes and warrant
furtDher evaluation.1215-1218
Kidney health protection strategies should include communication about possible
causes of AKI, how to maintain fluid balance during an acute illness, and when to seek
medical attention. Guidance on supporting medication management for adults during an
episode of acute illness requires an individualized risk assessment, considering a person’s
capacity to self-manage.1219-1221 Recommendations and practice points related to temporarily
withholding specific classes of drugs in the setting of acute illnesses that predispose to
hypovolemia or hypotension (SDMG) are provided in Chapter 4 (Section 4.11) and also
apply in the period following an episode of AKI or AKI. Patient self-management strategies
for SDMG should be provided in conjunction with support from healthcare professionals684,
1219 and reinforced during hospital discharge, follow-up visits, and transitions in care.
Given the association between AKI and long-term physical and cognitive impairment,
educational interventions should also address rehabilitation strategies, including promotion of
physical activity and nutritional support to preserve muscle mass and support
neurodevelopmental recovery. Pediatric AKI education programs should center the child and
family, with kidney health advocacy integrated into broader child health and non-
communicable disease initiatives.1222
330

Recommendation 6.1.2: We suggest risk stratification of AKI survivors at hospital
discharge using a validated tool to identify children and adults at high risk of CKD who
may benefit from nephrology care after AKI (2D).
This recommendation and related practice point place a high value on assessing a person’s
risk of severe CKD to determine the most appropriate form of follow-up care following
AKI/AKD, recognizing a need to ensure children and adults are seen by the appropriate
clinical professionals, in the right setting and at the right time according to their physical,
T
psychological, and social needs and preferences. The recommendation aims to optimize
resource allocation and minimize treatment burden, particularly in terms of who is likely to
benefit most from nephrology care following hospital discharge after AKI/AKD.
F
Key information
Balance of benefits and harms
A Canadian trial randomized adults with a history of Stage 2–3 AKI to receive a risk-
stratified intervention guided by a validated risk index to predict risk of severe CKD versus
A
usual post-discharge care (Supplementary Table S94).1223 In the risk stratified group, high-
risk participants received follow-up with a nephrologist and low-risk participants were
followed up by primary care physicians with recommendations provided. The trial found that
this strategy increased nephrology follow-up for those participants with sustained eGFR<30
R
ml/min per 1.73 m2 (RR: 3.17; 95% CI: 1.45–6.95).1223 There was also greater use of RASi
and statins when clinically indicated in the group at high risk of severe CKD among those
who received the risk-stratified follow-up intervention, but without evidence of differences in
development of CKD G5, need for long-term dialysis, or mortality (Supplementary Table
S92D).1224 When using a composite processes of care outcome consisting of nephrology
follow-up among people with CKD at 90 days post hospital discharge and adherence to the
management recommendations for ACEi/ARBs and statins, risk-based follow-up was
associated with significantly better care delivery (RR: 9.34; 95% CI: 2.58–43.62).1223
There are a number of ongoing RCTs in progress in North America evaluating the
clinical effectiveness of interventions to improve follow-up care after AKI.1123, 1205, 1225
Certainty of evidence
There is very low certainty of evidence, based on a single small study, that use of risk-
stratification to guide care improves some measures of quality of care (i.e., RASi and statin
use, nephrology follow-up) among adult survivors of AKI at high risk of severe CKD, but no
effect has been demonstrated on individual prioritized kidney-related clinical outcomes in
adults.1223 The certainty of evidence was downgraded for sparseness, imprecision, and
methodological limitations (lack of blinding).
331

Values and preferences
There is broad agreement that follow-up care after AKI/AKD should be
multiprofessional, with kidney health protection plans developed prior to hospital discharge
and tailored to people’s overall care needs. This recommendation places a high value on
optimizing care for AKI survivors with or at risk of CKD. Targeted follow-up for people at
higher risk of CKD following AKI makes efficient use of both nephrologist and primary care,
providing evidence-based therapies aimed at reducing kidney disease progression and
cardiovascular consequences, such as use of RASi, statins, and SGLT2i.1165, 1226 This
approach also aims to minimize the burden of follow-up (i.e., anxiety, financial, employment,
T
time) for low-risk patients and their caregivers.
Resource use and costs
There is insufficient capacity within the global nephrology workforces to see all AKI
F
survivors, with a need to largely place AKI in the context of enhancing comprehensive,
person-centered generalist care. A risk-stratified approach to follow-up, intensifying resource
use for those at highest risk of CKD, can optimize nephrology resource allocation and help
ensure people receive care from the appropriate team, in the right setting, and at the right time
A
without overburdening low-risk patients.
Considerations for implementation
Identification of people with AKI/AKD who benefit most from follow-up care after
R
hospital discharge remains challenging in clinical practice. A validation study of risk models
demonstrated that greater net benefit was achieved when follow-up decisions were based on a
risk prediction model for severe CKD after AKI rather than strategies based only on AKI
severity or kidney function at discharge.1227 In future, integration of risk calculation tools into
rouDtine hospital workflows may facilitate uptake but needs to be linked with proper training
and support for healthcare professionals to interpret risk scores and determine appropriate
follow-up care pathways. Resource allocation, such as ensuring timely access to nephrology
services for high-risk people, is also needed to facilitate timely care.
Risk models that accurately estimate the probability of severe CKD following AKI
are available to identify survivors of AKI who may benefit from nephrology care. James et
al. derived and externally validated a predictive model for advanced CKD (G4 or G5) after an
episode of AKI.1228 (Figure 21) The 6-variable model (including age, gender, baseline SCr,
albuminuria, AKI severity, and SCr at hospital discharge as predictors) was well calibrated
and achieved a C-statistic of 0.81 (95% CI: 0.75–0.86) in the validation cohort. 1228
332

T
F
Figure 21 | 6-variable risk score to predict transition to severe CKD (G4 or G5) for
adults after hospitalization withA AKI.1228 AKI, acute kidney injury; CKD, chronic kidney
disease
Clear communication strategies with patients about their risk status and the
importance of follow-up care are important. Ongoing monitoring of a risk model’s
R
performance and adherence to follow-up protocols can help refine processes, ensuring that
people most likely to benefit from nephrology care receive appropriate support.
Rationale
DThere is a significant gap between the need for nephrology care and the available
global nephrology workforce.1229 Given the high global incidence of AKI, it is not feasible
for every AKI survivor to receive AKI care from a nephrologist, therefore identification of
people most likely to benefit from nephrology care optimizes the use of these limited
resources. Fewer than 5% of adults with AKI see a nephrologist after hospital discharge,
including <30% of those who subsequently develop CKD within a year after
hospitalization.1230 Nephrology follow-up after pediatric AKI is also infrequent, even among
those with severe AKI, despite known risks for CKD and hypertension.1176, 1231
Many of the recommendations for follow-up of AKI survivors can be effectively
provided by non-nephrology healthcare teams, including primary care in collaboration with
allied healthcare professionals (pharmacists, nurses, dieticians, social workers, social-
prescribing teams, community health workers). However, it is likely that some people may
specifically benefit from nephrology care, particularly those at high risk for major adverse
kidney outcomes (e.g., de novo CKD or CKD progression). Aligning use of nephrology
services to a person’s risk of developing severe CKD has the potential to reduce treatment
burden for low-risk patients and better optimize nephrology follow-up care for those with
greatest need. Reducing the intensity of follow-up for low-risk patients acknowledges that not
333

all people develop CKD and that adults may encounter barriers to follow-up care, including
hospitalization related-fatigue, a reluctance to see more doctors, and long travel times.1167, 1169
Most patients receive follow-up with other physicians, indicating opportunities for
knowledge translation and surveillance to be integrated into subspecialty care or general
medical clinics for people at low risk of severe CKD. Given that nearly all AKI survivors
engage with the healthcare system post discharge, targeted knowledge translation and
implementation of pragmatic follow-up strategies, especially for non-nephrologists, are
essential to ensure appropriate long-term kidney monitoring and intervention.
T
6.2. Medication management following AKI and/or AKD
Recommendation 6.2.1: We recommend starting or resuming RASi in adults, unless
there is a contraindication, in accordance with evidence-based guideline indications
F
(adults: 1B; children: 1D).
This recommendation places a high value on the benefits of evidence-based therapies to treat
diabetes, cardiovascular disease (including heart failure), and CKD; all of which are
A
common comorbidities in children and adults with AKI and may also contribute to the
development of AKI. Observational studies report better survival among adults resuming or
starting these medications after an episode of AKI and/or AKD. This recommendation aligns
with the KDIGO 2024 Clinical Practice Guideline for the Evaluation and Management of
R
CKD (Sections 3.6) for specific recommendations and practice points in relation to RASi.1164
Although there is very little evidence in children, the Work Group felt this recommendation
would also apply to children.
Key information
D
Balance of benefits and harms
The effectiveness of RASi treatment as a strategy to prevent adverse outcomes after
AKI/AKD has not been evaluated in randomized trials; however observational studies have
reported lower mortality among people who resumed these medications than those who did
not following a hospitalization with AKI. Some observational studies have shown that
treatment with RASi may increase the risk of hyperkalemia and readmission for AKI. In a
randomized trial from Canada where more patients in the intervention arm received RASi
after a hospitalization with AKI, there was a higher rate of hyperkalemia in the intervention
arm, suggesting that close laboratory monitoring is required for safety.
An evidence review of 8 studies including 81,869 adults demonstrated that reinitiation
of ACEi or ARBs after AKI was associated with a significantly lower mortality (HR: 0.83;
95% CI:0.75–0.91) and MACE (HR: 0.88; 95% CI: 0.85–0.92) without increasing the risk of
recurrent AKI or MAKE (Supplementary Table S95).1187, 1188, 1232-1237 Across 5 studies
including 31,970 adults, newly starting ACEi or ARBs after AKI was also associated with
significantly lower mortality (HR: 0.87; 95% CI: 0.81–0.95; Supplementary Table S96).1165,
1233, 1235-1237
334

A multicenter retrospective cohort analysis from Taiwan evaluated the timing of
RASi reinitiation after an AKI episode and observed that earlier reinitiation of these
medications in adults within 3 months of discharge was associated with a lower hazard for
mortality, cardiovascular events, and kidney failure, without an increased risk of severe
hyperkalemia.1238 The benefit of resuming these therapies was also seen among adult
participants with an eGFR <30 ml/min per 1.73 m2 where earlier initiation was associated
with a lower risk of dialysis or kidney failure. Other observational studies have reported
lowest mortality when RASi was started in the initial month after AKI among adults with
diabetes and proteinuria, regardless of whether the medication was started before or after
T
resolution of AKI.1014
Certainty of evidence
There is moderate certainty of evidence that reinitiation of ACEi or ARBs after an
F
episode of AKI reduces the likelihood of death and MACE, with no evidence of effects on
likelihood of recurrent AKI, other AKI-related conditions, or poor control of underlying
conditions other than MACE in adults. The certainty of evidence was downgraded for
methodological quality of nonrandomized studies.1187, 1188, 1232-1237
A
There is moderate certainty of evidence that newly starting ACEi or ARBs after an
episode of AKI reduces the likelihood of death, with low to moderate certainty of evidence of
no evidence of effects on likelihood of AKI-related conditions in adults. Studies did not
R
report on outcomes related to poor control of underlying conditions. The certainty of
evidence was downgraded for methodological quality of nonrandomized studies and
imprecisions of some AKI-related outcomes.1165, 1233, 1235-1237
ValDues and preferences
This recommendation places a high value on preventing future adverse health
outcomes in AKI/AKD survivors. Diabetes, cardiovascular disease, heart failure, and CKD
are common comorbidities in people who experience AKI and may contribute to recurrent
AKI; ensuring that these conditions are effectively managed after an AKI episode is a key
priority and is associated with better health outcomes.
Resource use and costs
Starting or restarting these therapies may involve short-term costs for people with
respect to medication prescription and monitoring. However, this must be measured against
long-term costs to patients and healthcare systems related to rehospitalization, cardiovascular
morbidity and kidney events that may be potentially avoided.
Considerations for implementation
We recognize that care gaps exist in the treatment of chronic health conditions
associated with AKI in adults and in particular in children. Further, the availability of
healthcare professionals to recognize care gaps and address them is challenging in many parts
of the world. Where possible, medication review and reconciliation should be integrated into
routine follow-up care for people with AKI/AKD. Healthcare professional education is
335

required to inform medication prescribers of the risks and benefits of starting or restarting
RASi, and how to use them safely.
Rationale
Conditions benefiting from the use of RASi are prevalent in children and adults with a
history of AKI/AKD. RASi are frequently stopped during an AKI event because of their
effects on kidney hemodynamics and GFR, their association with hypotension, and the risk of
hyperkalemia. In many patients, these drugs are not restarted after hospital discharge, despite
clinal indications to do so, which is a potential gap in care.1162, 1213
T
Numerous retrospective cohort studies have evaluated the associations between ACEi
or ARBs and clinical outcomes of people with AKI/AKD.1239 A systematic review and meta-
analysis of 7 retrospective cohort studies in adults reported lower all-cause mortality among
F
ACEi or ARB users, including those who newly started or continued use after an AKI
episode (OR: 0.37; 95% CI: 0.42 to 0.32) as well as an increased risk of hyperkalemia,
however, there is an absence of evidence from RCTs on the effect of RASi initiation or
reinitiation on other important clinical outcomes after an episode of AKI. Further, there is an
A
absence of RCTs in children.
Practice Point 6.2.1: Evaluate the clinical indication for starting or resuming sodium-
glucose cotransporter-2 inhibitors (SGLT2i), glucagon-like peptide-1 receptor agonists
R
(GLP-1 RA) and mineralocorticoid receptor antagonists (MRA) in children and adults.
SGLT2i are indicated for use in children and adults with type 2 diabetes (T2D), CKD,
and heart failure; all are common conditions among people who experience AKI. GLP-1 RAs
alsoD have been shown to have important effects on clinical outcomes in adults with T2D and
CKD. These medications are frequently stopped during an episode of AKI due to a concern
that they may exacerbate AKI due to their diuretic effects (SGLT2i) or gastrointestinal effects
on oral intake (GLP-1 RA). Large retrospective cohort studies from Taiwan reported better
outcomes with the use of SGLT2i and GLP-1 RA in people with diabetes and AKD.1240 1241
These studies report a lower risk of all-cause mortality, major adverse kidney outcomes, and
major adverse cardiac outcomes among people with clinical indications for restarting
treatment.
MRAs are also commonly prescribed in populations at risk for AKI and are frequently
stopped due to their association with hyperkalemia. Less is known about restarting MRA
after an AKI event; however, given the range of demonstrated benefits over these disease
states, cautious reinitiation of these medications after AKI in those with a clinical indication
for treatment, no contraindications, stable kidney function, and an eGFR >25 ml/min per 1.73
m2 should be considered.
336

Practice Point 6.2.2: Assess the appropriateness of starting or resuming guideline-
indicated medications at hospital discharge and by 3 months after AKI/AKD.
The optimal timing to restart medications after AKI/AKD is unknown. Reinitiation of
medications should be considered in the overall context of the person and individualized
based on comorbidities, resolution of acute illness, clinical assessment, and kidney
function.574 Diuretics and antihypertensive medications should be restarted when clinically
indicated. Persistent electrolyte abnormalities or non-recovery of kidney function may
preclude starting or restarting medications known to affect electrolyte balance, particularly
T
those that affect serum potassium levels (i.e., MRA, RASi). If indicated, these medications
should be restarted when kidney function and electrolyte balance stabilize, with a plan for
frequent monitoring.
F
Practice Point 6.2.3: Monitor RASi, MRA, or diuretic prescription and management
following AKI/AKD in accordance with the KDIGO 2024 Clinical Practice Guideline
for the Evaluation and Management of Chronic Kidney Disease.
A
The Work Group agrees with recommendations and selected practice points from the
KDIGO 2024 Clinical Practice Guideline for the Evaluation and Management of CKD for the
treatment of CKD with RASi, SGLT2i, GLP-1 RA, and MRA.1164 This guideline provides
specific information about drug dosing and monitoring of potassium and kidney function,
R
after treatment initiation that apply to resumption following an episode of AKI/AKD.
6.3 Evaluation for CKD
Practice Point 6.3.1: Assess kidney function and markers of kidney damage 3 months
afteDr AKI or AKD to evaluate for resolution, and new onset or worsening of preexisting
CKD.
Episodes of AKI and/or AKD are predictors for new or progressive CKD and kidney
failure in children and adults, therefore assessments of both kidney function (SCr or cystatin
C, eGFR, electrolytes) and kidney damage (UACR or urinary protein-to-creatinine ratio
[UPCR]) 3 months after an AKI/AKD episode are important.54, 57, 192 Furthermore, both
reduced eGFR and albuminuria at 3 months are associated with subsequent progression of
kidney disease.57, 207, 528, 1150, 1242
Recognizing that AKI, AKD, and CKD are related entities, monitoring for the
development and progression of CKD is essential to improve kidney care and outcomes
across this continuum of health states (Figure 22).59, 528 Children and adults identified with
CKD following AKI or AKD should be managed as detailed in the KDIGO 2024 Clinical
Practice Guideline for the Evaluation and Management of CKD.1164 Children and adults
without CKD at 3 months remain at increased risk for new onset CKD and should also be
managed according to the KDIGO 2024 Clinical Practice Guideline for the Evaluation and
Management of CKD (Practice Point 1.1.1.1). Determining if, when, and how to initiate;
restart; and uptitrate medication of clinical prognostic benefit for people with or at risk of
337

CKD after an episode of AKI and/or AKD requires careful consideration, with assessment of
eGFR, UACR, and electrolytes.
T
F
Figure 22 | Proposed conceptual model for the continuum of AKD, CKD, and AKI.59
AKD, acute kidney disease; AKI, acute kidney injury; CKD, chronic kidney disease
A
Practice Point 6.3.2: Serum cystatin C-based GFR estimation can be used to assess for
CKD in children and adults after AKI in clinical situations where SCr is less accurate.
Muscle wasting is a common complication of prolonged hospitalization and is
R
particularly prevalent in critical illness as well as other conditions commonly seen in people
with AKI including cancer, malnutrition, heart failure, and cirrhosis.1243 Cystatin C may be
more accurate for estimating GFR than SCr among people who have had prolonged or critical
illness and/or experienced significant weight loss or sarcopenia. The Work Group also refers
to rDecommendations and selected practice points from the KDIGO 2024 Clinical Practice
Guideline for the Evaluation and Management of CKD for the use of eGFRcr-cys.1164
In the context of hospitalized AKI, the use of SCr as a measure of kidney function and
resolution of AKI may be confounded by reduction in muscle mass and changes in creatinine
generation. This may result in an overestimation of kidney recovery in people with
AKI/AKD.20, 1017, 1243-1248 Cystatin C is independent of muscle mass and may be a better
marker of kidney function in these people.
A prospective single center study of critically ill patients in the UK sequentially
assessed kidney function using cystatin C, SCr, and measured GFR using iohexol, and found
that eGFR by SCr systematically overestimated kidney function.13 The AKI Risk in Derby
(ARID) cohort study in the UK also demonstrated large discrepancies between eGFR based
on cystatin C and eGFR based on SCr in a general hospitalized population at 3 months
following discharge, with the magnitude in difference being more prominent in people
affected by AKI.1249
338

6.4 Post-discharge care for children and adults receiving RRT following AKI in the
outpatient setting
Practice Point 6.4.1: RRT programs should incorporate comprehensive dialysis care
and communication plans for children and adults receiving RRT following AKI tailored
to their likelihood of dialysis liberation.
Up to 30% of adults who receive RRT for AKI and survive to hospital discharge will
remain dialysis dependent at discharge, and of these, approximately half will be able to
discontinue dialysis within 90 days.1250 People who need ongoing RRT after hospitalization
T
with AKI/AKD report inconsistent care in outpatient dialysis facilities where their care is
frequently not distinguished from those receiving long-term dialysis for kidney failure.
However, care goals for people receiving RRT after AKI often differ significantly from those
receiving maintenance dialysis for chronic kidney failure, as recovery of kidney function is a
F
desired outcome.
A specialized approach to care for people receiving short-term RRT in the outpatient
setting may benefit patients, healthcare professionals, and healthcare systems. Patients may
A
benefit from avoiding unnecessary harm of prolonged, unnecessary RRT if recovery of
kidney function is identified. Those with a greater probability of resolution may benefit from
additional monitoring procedures to identify recovery of kidney function and avoid
prolonged, unnecessary RRT. Identification of people with a low likelihood of resolution may
R
assist them, their caregivers, healthcare professionals, and healthcare systems in care
planning discussions for long-term RRT and aid planning and resource allocation within
dialysis units.
DPrehospitalization kidney function is a strong predictor of dialysis independence;
however, up to 30% of patients with baseline CKD G4 may also achieve dialysis
independence post discharge.812, 1251 Some predictive models have been published to better
stratify the likelihood of discontinuing dialysis in adults following AKI.1252
Lee et al. developed and validated a prediction model for kidney recovery after AKI
in a cohort of 2214 adults who started dialysis in hospital.1252 Four factors were identified
through stepwise regression as independent predictors of dialysis liberation: preadmission
eGFR, preadmission hemoglobin, history of chronic liver disease, and age. This model
excluded people with a >20% risk of inpatient mortality, only included preadmission and
intra-admission variables, and did not account for the competing risk of death. It was also not
externally validated and had relatively modest discrimination (C-index: 0.64).
Clark et al. developed and externally validated a predictive score for kidney recovery
and death in patients continuing dialysis as outpatients after starting in hospital using a
population based cohort of 7657 patient in Ontario Canada.1253 Predictive variables identified
in this analysis included comorbidities, days from admission to dialysis, days from first
dialysis to discharge, ICU status, prehospital admission eGFR, and random UACR. While the
ability of this tool to discriminate was modest (C-statistic: 0.7; 95% CI: 0.67–0.73 and 0.66;
339

95% CI: 0.62–0.69 for the outcomes of recovery and death, respectively), the risk groups
identified in the study did allow a degree of prognostic separation that may be clinically
useful.
Practice Point 6.4.2: Engage patients, caregivers, and outpatient dialysis care teams in
the development of a post-discharge care plan prior to transition from the hospital to
community settings.
Practice Point 6.4.3: Dialysis care for children and adults receiving RRT following AKI
T
requires clinical assessment of volume status, laboratory evaluation for evidence of
kidney recovery, medication review and reconciliation, and patient education about
identification of return of kidney function and minimizing kidney injury during the
resolution phase.
F
Practice Point 6.4.4: Provide education and engage adults, children, and caregivers in
shared decision-making focused around self-care, short- and long-term risks of
permanent RRT and mortality, quality of life, and goals of care to inform decisions
A
around dialysis care.
Transition to outpatient RRT requires timely transmission of discharge summaries,
medication review and reconciliation, and recognition of potential for kidney function
R
recovery to inform an appropriate monitoring plans. A post-discharge plan should engage
patients, caregivers, and dialysis care professionals with clear roles and responsibilities
(Table 59). Patient and caregiver education should be developmentally appropriate, culturally
sensitive, and include individualized discussions on expectations for resolution, long-term
risks, and care preferences.
D

342

Structured clinical evaluation should include assessment of volume status, BP, and
medication review as well as assessment of dialysis adequacy and kidney recovery.
Assessment of kidney function should occur at regular intervals, at least weekly during the
first month. The WATCH-ME framework incorporates many of these elements (Table 60).791,
1210
Intradialytic hypotension and hemodynamic instability may delay or impair recovery
of kidney function in people with AKI.1254 There is no specific BP target for people with AKI
receiving dialysis, however it may be reasonable to tolerate a slightly higher BP when there is
T
potential for resolution to minimize the risk of renal hypoperfusion. Similarly, volume
management should aim to minimize the risk of hypovolemia, intradialytic hypotension, and
organ hypoperfusion, while avoiding symptomatic volume overload. Outpatients receiving
RRT following AKI may experience dynamic changes in lean body mass and volume due to
F
prolonged illness, recovery from illness, and changing kidney function, which can
additionally affect dietary intake. Assessment of BP and volume (including weight) is
required with each dialysis treatment. Diuretics may be helpful in managing volume and are
associated with less risk of intradialytic hypotension.761, 1255
A
Medication review and management is recommended for all people transitioning to
the outpatient dialysis setting following AKI. Patients and caregivers should be instructed and
educated to avoid nephrotoxin exposures and be provided sick day guidance. Pharmacist-
R
assisted medication management may reduce the risk of nephrotoxin exposure and lower
rates of rehospitalization in AKI survivors.1171, 1256 The optimal timing for initiating or
resuming cardio- and renoprotective medications (e.g., ACEi, statins, SGLT2i) in adults with
AKI who are still receiving RRT post hospitalization is unclear.
D

345

The criteria for and processes by which dialysis may be discontinued for people
following AKI is covered in Chapter 5 and has not been specifically studied for people with
AKI receiving outpatient dialysis. Most people with AKI who successfully discontinue
dialysis in the outpatient setting will have or will be at risk for CKD. Patients should be
educated about this risk, as well as the risk of recurrent AKI, and general aspects of kidney
care, as outlined in Section 6.1.
Practice Point 6.4.5: Upon discontinuation of outpatient dialysis, a nephrologist should
conduct a comprehensive assessment within 7 days, followed by an additional
T
evaluation of kidney function at 90 days.
Discontinuation of dialysis represents another transition in the care and period of
vulnerability for people with AKI who have been treated with RRT. Patients should be
F
assessed by a nephrologist with the timing and frequency of follow-up determined by the
person’s clinical status at the time of dialysis discontinuation. While assessment should occur
at a minimum of 7 days after dialysis discontinuation, volume and electrolyte disturbances
may require closer and more freqAuent assessment. All recovering patients, regardless of the
degree of resolution should have an assessment of kidney function at 90 days to assess for the
presence of de novo or progressive CKD.
The majority of adults receiving RRT for AKI will recover sufficiently to discontinue
R
dialysis within 90 days People still requiring dialysis after this period of time should be
.
reassessed with respect to prognosis. The goals of care should be discussed and agreed with
the patient and caregiver, and additional patient education should be provided to inform their
transition to long-term dialysis for kidney failure.
D
6.5. Considerations specific to children
6.5.1 Planning follow-up care for children after AKI and/or AKD
Practice Point 6.5.1.1: Assess children following AKI for global functional status at
hospital discharge and for growth parameters and functional status at outpatient
follow-up.
The sequelae of AKI in children can include growth restriction, functional decline,
neurodevelopmental impairment, and reduced health-related quality of life,1086, 1087, 1257-1259 in
addition to hypertension and CKD. These outcomes reinforce the importance of assessments
of growth parameters and functional metrics in post-AKI care of children, in addition to BP
and laboratory monitoring. Post-AKI care in children requires careful interpretation of kidney
function changes in the context of hemodynamic alterations. Rises in serum creatinine after
initiation of RASi can reflect hemodynamic changes rather than intrinsic kidney injury and
should not prompt medication discontinuation. As in adult patients, kidney function testing in
children should be performed with close attention to the clinical picture and volume status.
346

Follow-up plans for children after AKI may vary by age, AKI severity, duration,
degree of resolution, and comorbid risk (Table 61). High-risk infants, such as those with
Stage 2–3 AKI, persistent AKI (>48 hours), or residual proteinuria, require comprehensive
lab evaluation, BP monitoring, and growth tracking, beginning within 3 months post-
discharge. Adolescents with AKI should also receive counseling on NSAID avoidance,
lifestyle risks, and reproductive health, ideally as part of transition planning. Growth
parameters, including head circumference in infants, should be systematically incorporated
into post-AKI follow-up given that AKI in infancy and early childhood is associated with
impaired growth. Colocation of AKI follow-up assessment with other subspecialty clinics
T
may reduce caregiver burden.
In the largest multicenter cohort to date evaluating functional outcomes after children
receiving CRRT for AKI or fluid overload, functional decline was common and often
F
persisted beyond hospital discharge.1257 Discharge Functional Status Scale (FSS) scores were
the most robust predictor of long-term outcome, highlighting the need for early, proximal
assessments to trigger intervention. These findings support incorporating functional
assessment tools such as the FSS, and Pediatric Quality of Life Inventory (PedsQL) into
A
discharge planning and post-AKI surveillance. Children with elevated FSS at discharge,
particularly those with sepsis, may benefit from early referral to developmental and
rehabilitation services. Pediatric AKI follow-up should encompass targeted functional
support and neurodevelopmental interventions, especially for high-risk populations such as
R
preterm infants, those requiring CRRT, and survivors of septic shock.
D
347

349

Practice Point 6.5.1.2: In children previously taking RASi, these medications should be
resumed with resolution of AKI and/or AKD, unless there is a contraindication.
The American Academy of Pediatrics recommends that children and adolescents with
CKD, hypertension, and proteinuria should be treated with RASi.1260 According to the
KDIGO 2025 IgA Nephropathy and IgA Vasculitis Guideline, all children with IgA
nephropathy and proteinuria >200 mg/d or UPCR >200 mg/g (>20 mg/mmol) should receive
RAS blockade.1261 RASi are also commonly indicated for pediatric cardiovascular conditions;
the American Heart Association 2024 scientific statement on chronic heart failure in children
T
and adolescents with congenital heart disease notes that RASi may be considered in children
and adolescents with congenital heart disease and systemic left ventricular dysfunction,
particularly in the presence of hypertension.1262 In a 2024 randomized, double-blind trial of
375 children with heart failure due to systemic left ventricular systolic dysfunction,
F
sacubitril/valsartan did not differ significantly from enalapril at 52 weeks using a global rank
endpoint (Mann–Whitney probability 0.52; p=0.42), with both arms demonstrating
improvements in functional status and biomarkers and similar safety profiles.1263 Temporary
discontinuation of RASi during episodes of AKI and/or AKD in these pediatric populations
A
and others where the benefit from RASi may exist should not preclude resumption once
clinical stability is achieved. As in adults, careful clinical assessment and laboratory
monitoring are essential to ensure safe reintroduction and to avoid missed opportunities for
long-term organ protection.
R
6.5.2. Post-discharge care for children receiving RRT following AKI in the outpatient
setting
Practice Point 6.5.2.1: A risk-based approach guided by cause and severity of AKI and
kidDney health assessments at hospital discharge and one year following an AKI episode
can be used to determine need for future nephrology follow-up in children.
A limited pediatric nephrology workforce and disparities in access to specialized care
can adversely impact the delivery of care for children following AKI. A tiered follow-up
framework informed by AKI severity and the context and cause of the AKI episode, with
nephrology referral and surveillance for children with Stage 2–3 AKI, delayed kidney
recovery, or predisposing conditions such as congenital anomalies of the kidney and urinary
tract or other primary kidney diseases has been proposed.288 A prospective study showed that
children who demonstrate normal kidney function in the first year following an episode of
AKI were at low risk of developing CKD within the subsequent 2–4 years.1264 The risk of
CKD was lowest when preserved eGFR was combined with absence of proteinuria and
hypertension. These findings support a risk-based approach to post-AKI follow-up in which
children with a normal kidney health assessment at one year without complex comorbidities
or subsequent AKI episodes may be safely discharged from pediatric nephrology care,
allowing for more efficient allocation of resources and tailored long-term surveillance (Table
62)
350

351

Practice Point 6.5.2.2: Children recovering from AKI treated with RRT require
individualized follow-up that includes age-appropriate functional and developmental
assessments, growth and neurodevelopmental surveillance, and integrated care across
nephrology, primary care, and relevant subspecialties.
There is evidence of a rising incidence of pediatric AKI requiring RRT, with
population-based data from Canada showing an increase from 0.58 to 0.65 per 1,000 person-
years between 1996 and 2015.1109 Over this period, there haTs been a notable shift in modality
use: the relative use of PD has declined, while both intermittent hemodialysis and CRRT
have increased.1109, 1218 These trends likely reflect both increased illness severity and broader
access to advanced extracorporeal technologies. Among survivors of AKI treated with RRT,
up to 70% exhibit subclinical evidence of kidneFy damage, such as proteinuria, hypertension,
and reduced eGFR after dialysis is discontinued.1017 This underscores the importance of
structured, longitudinal follow-up that includes regular assessment of BP and kidney
function.
A
The majority of children who survive after RRT for AKI, recover kidney function,
but rates vary by illness severity, modality, and underlying condition.1265 In an international,
multicenter cohort study of critically ill children treated with CRRT, 9.4% remained dialysis-
dependent at 90 daRys.1088 Resolution typically occurs within 2–6 weeks after AKI onset, but
delayed or incomplete resolution is more common in children with multisystem organ
dysfunction, preexisting CKD, onco-hematologic conditions, or significant nephrotoxin
exposure. The EurAKId registry, a prospective European cohort, found that 10%–30% of
children treated with RRT for AKI experienced delayed resolution or transitioned to dialysis
D
dependence, especially in high-risk subgroups.1038
Recovery of kidney function after RRT may also occur in the outpatient setting. A
retrospective analysis of the United States Renal Data System showed that resolution was
more frequent among younger children, females, non-White patients, and those initially
treated with hemodialysis.1266 Children with acute tubular necrosis had the highest resolution
rates (17.5%), reinforcing the importance of tailoring expectations and follow-up intensity
based on the underlying etiology of AKI. These data support cautious use of the kidney
failure label in children with potentially reversible causes of kidney injury, emphasizing the
need for vigilant surveillance of residual kidney function over time.
Optimal care for children recovering from AKI treated with RRT should include
comprehensive clinical and laboratory assessment of kidney recovery, regular medication
review and reconciliation with attention to nephrotoxic exposures, and patient and caregiver
education on recognizing signs of resolution or deterioration. Nutritional and fluid
management goals must account for growth and developmental changes, with dynamic
352

adjustments throughout resolution. Care models should facilitate coordination between
intensive care, pediatric nephrology, primary care, and rehabilitation services to address the
complex needs of these children. Given the limited pediatric nephrology workforce and
potential barriers to specialty care, centralized or telehealth-enabled follow-up could be
considered. Additionally, educating general pediatricians and relevant subspecialists on post-
AKI surveillance and criteria for nephrology referral can enhance the safety and continuity of
care during the vulnerable resolution phase.
T
Pediatric survivors of AKI or early-onset CKD who progress to kidney failure in
childhood and adolescence experience enduring disruptions in social, emotional, and
functional domains during young adulthood. While many adapt to chronic illness, the
cumulative burden of kidney injury early in lifeF, including developmental impacts, treatment
intensity, and transplant challenges, manifests in constrained life participation. Tracking
employment, independence, and health-related quality of life metrics into adulthood are also
essential aspects of pediatric AKI follow-up.1267
A
R
D
353

METHODS FOR GUIDELINE DEVELOPMENT
Aim
The aim of this project was to update the KDIGO 2012 Clinical Practice Guideline
for Acute Kidney Injury.608 The guideline update development methods are described
below.
T
Overview of process
These guidelines adhered to international best practices for guideline development
(Appendix B: Supplementary Tables S2, S3, and S4),1268, 1269 and have been reported in
accordance with the Institute of Medicine andF Appraisal of Guidelines for Research and
Evaluation (AGREE) II reporting checklists.1270 The processes undertaken for the
development of the KDIGO 2026 Clinical Practice Guideline for Acute Kidney Injury
(AKI) and Acute Kidney Disease (AKD) are described below.
A
• Appointing Work Group members and the Evidence Review Team (ERT)
• Defining scope and the topics of interest for the guideline
• Developing and refining topics for systematic evidence review
R
• Developing and implementing literature search strategies
• Selecting studies according to predefined eligibility criteria
• Conducting data extraction and risk of bias assessment of the included studies
• Conducting evidence syntheses and meta-analysis, where appropriate
D• Assessing the certainty of evidence for each outcome across studies, and assessing
the overall certainty of evidence across critical outcomes
• Finalizing guideline recommendations, practice points, and supporting rationale
• Grading the strength of recommendations, based on the overall certainty of
evidence and other considerations
• Convening a public review of the guideline draft in March 2026
• Amending the guideline based on the external review feedback and updated
systematic reviews
• Finalizing and publishing the guideline
Commissioning of Work Group and ERT for the guideline update
For the guideline update, KDIGO and the Co-Chairs assembled and engaged a
Work Group with expertise in adult and pediatric nephrology, critical care medicine,
emergency medicine, general medicine, pharmacy and pharmacology, as well as people
with lived experience of AKI. The Brown University Center for Evidence Synthesis in
Health in Providence, Rhode Island which has expertise in nephrology, evidence synthesis,
and guideline development was contracted as the ERT and was tasked with conducting and
354

updating the systematic reviews. The ERT coordinated the methodological and analytical
processes of guideline development, including literature searching, data extraction, risk of
bias assessment, evidence synthesis and meta-analysis, grading the certainty of the
evidence for each critical and important outcome, and grading the overall certainty of the
evidence for the recommendations. KDIGO Methods Committee representatives also
ensured the guideline development process adhered to the KDIGO methods as outlined in
the KDIGO Methods Manual and provided guidance of guideline methodology throughout
the development of the guideline. The Work Group was reTsponsible for defining the scope
of the guideline, writing the recommendations and the underlying supporting text, grading
the strength of the recommendations, and developing practice points.
Defining scope and formulating key clinicalF questions
The KDIGO 2012 AKI Guideline was reviewed by the Co-Chairs to establish a
framework for the 2026 update. A complete Scope of Work encompassing both legacy
topics and newly identified clinical topics for inclusion in the update was revised and
A
finalized based on feedback from the Work Group and external review. Based on this list of
topics, the ERT suggested topics for systematic evidence review to inform the development
of evidence-based recommendations. In an iterative process, with additional discussion
with Work Group members, the list of systematic review topics was finalized, and study
eligibility criteriRa were determined based on the Population, Intervention, Comparator,
Outcomes, and Study design (PICOS) formulation. A shortlist of potential outcomes of
interest was derived from a 2019 National Institute of Diabetes and Digestive and Kidney
Diseases (NIDDK) workshop,1161 a core outcome set for multimorbidity research,1271 and
the ERT experience with kidney guidelines. Outcome prioritization was based on
D
consideration of the Standardized Outcomes in Nephrology (SONG) outcome sets
(https://songinitiative.org/); although SONG has not published an outcome set specific to
AKI.
Details of the PICOS questions are provided with prioritized outcomes noted in
Table 63. For all topics, relevant existing systematic reviews of eligible primary studies
were also identified. All evidence reviews were conducted in accordance with the Cochrane
Handbook,1272 the Agency for Healthcare Research and Quality Evidence-based Practice
Center Program Methods Guide,1273 and guideline development adhered to the standards of
GRADE.1274
355
