Literature search and article selection
Searches were conducted in MEDLINE (via PubMed) and Embase from 2013 (after
publication of the KDIGO 2012 AKI Guideline) through January to June 2025, on a rolling
basis, with an update during public review in March/April 2026. The search strategies for
all databases are provided in Appendix A: Supplementary Tables S1. Throughout the
systematic review and guideline development process, additional studies suggested by the
Work Group were assessed for eligibility and incorporated as appropriate.
T
The unique titles and abstracts resulting from the searches were screened in
duplicate by members of the ERT, using the web-based screening tools SRDRplus or the
VADRR platform (https://vadrr.org/), which use large language models to sort abstracts
based on likely relevance. To simplify the scrFeening process, topics were batched by
similarity and reviewed in batches. Once the team established a shared consensus through
initial group reviews, the remaining abstracts were screened in duplicate, with adjudication
of conflicts by the whole team. The process for each batch ended either when the batch was
A
fully screened or when 400 consecutive abstracts were rejected suggesting a <1% chance of
finding more relevant studies. All potentially relevant studies were retrieved in full text and
rescreened in duplicate, with full team adjudication of conflicts.
For topicRs with high quality existing systematic reviews, the ERT sought additional
eligible primary studies published subsequent to the review in most cases and also included
other eligible studies found to have been omitted by the reviews. As noted in Table 63,
existing systematic reviews of biomarkers used to predict AKI (RQ 4) or comparisons of
intravenous fluids (RQ 6) were not updated.
D
The search identified 34,835 individual citations (Figure 23), if which1174 were
identified for full-text screening, plus an additional 79 additional records suggested by
Work Group members or found in reference lists of existing systematic reviews or
guidelines. Ultimately, 355 studies, including both primary studies and systematic reviews,
were included. These include 72 for Chapter 1, 59 for Chapter 2, 108 for Chapter 3, 57 for
Chapter 4, 37 for Chapter 5, and 22 for Chapter 6.
368

T
F
A
R
D
Figure 23 | Search yield and study flow diagram. C, comparator; D, study design; E,
exposure(s); ESR, existing systematic review; I, intervention(s); O, outcome(s); P,
population
Data extraction
Data extraction, from primary studies and existing systematic reviews, was
performed by one ERT member and confirmed by a second member of the ERT. Any
differences among members of the ERT were resolved through discussion. A third reviewer
was included if consensus could not be achieved. For some topics with existing systematic
reviews that did not provide sufficiently complete reporting of study descriptions or results
data or did not evaluate prioritized outcomes that were likely to have been reported, the
ERT sought the primary studies and extracted from these.
369

Risk of bias of studies
Studies were assessed for risk of bias and methodological concerns. The ERT used
the Cochrane Risk of Bias tool to evaluate risk of bias for RCTs1275 based on the risk of
selection bias, performance bias, detection bias, attrition bias, reporting bias, and other
potential biases.
For nonrandomized, observational comparative studies of interventions, pertinent
questions from the Cochrane Risk of Bias tool pertaining Tto outcome assessor blinding,
incomplete outcome data, and selective reporting were used as were selected questions
from the Risk Of Bias In Non-Randomized Studies - of Interventions (ROBINS-I) tool.1276
Specifically, comparative studies were evaluated as to whether evaluated cohorts were
comparable and whether potential confoundeFrs were accounted for.
For all intervention studies, including single group studies, it was determined
whether analyses were intention-to-treat were per-protocol, whether selection of
A
participants into the study was based on participant characteristics observed after the start
of intervention, selective reporting, whether there was clear reporting without
discrepancies, clear eligibility criteria, adequately described interventions, and adequate
outcome definition. For all studies, other potential biases or methodological problems of
note were also caRptured. Where methodological issues may have pertained only to some
reported outcomes, this was noted.
Risk of bias of diagnostic test studies was assessed using the Quality Assessment of
Diagnostic Accuracy Studies-1 (QUADAS 2) tool1277 with selected questions pertaining to
D
sample selection, blinding, prespecified methods, and completeness of data and analyses.
For association studies that evaluated potential predictors of outcomes, risk of bias was
assessed based on completeness and appropriateness of sample selection and analyses,
definitions of predictors and outcomes, completeness of reporting, and other methodologic
and reporting issues, all of which were notated.
Risk of bias for existing systematic reviews was assessed based on the Assessment
of Multiple Systematic Reviews 2 (AMSTAR 2) quality assessment tool.1278 Systematic
reviews were consider to be of high quality if at least 2 electronic databases were searched
(including either MEDLINE or Embase); there was a clear description of study eligibility
criteria, duplicate screening, explicit check of extracted data by another researcher, risk of
bias assessment; and appropriate meta-analysis methods were used. Systematic reviews that
were of poor quality solely due to poor meta-analysis methods, but were otherwise eligible,
were maintained but all pertinent meta-analyses were conducted again.
370

All risk of bias assessments were conducted independently by 2 members of the
ERT, with disagreements resolved by internal discussion and consultation with a third ERT
member, as needed.
Evidence synthesis and meta-analysis
Data for each topic was presented to the Work Group in summary tables describing
study characteristics, interventions (or prediction factors), outcomes, results data, summary
results, and risk of bias. T
Measures of treatment effect – All evaluated outcomes of interventions were
dichotomous. Thus, results that were summarized or meta-analyzed by the ERT were
expressed as RRs or HRs with 95% CIs. ResuFlts summarized by existing systematic
reviews were extracted in the metric reported (e.g., OR) and were typically not converted to
RRs. Analyses of associations were extracted in the reported metrics, preferentially
including the most-adjusted analyses. For diagnostic test studies, the ERT extracted
A
reported measures of test accuracy, including sensitivity and specificity, receiver operating
characteristic area under the curve, and likelihood ratios.
Data synthesis - Meta-analysis was conducted if there were 3 or more studies that
were sufficientlyR similar with respect to key variables (population characteristics, study
duration, comparisons). The ERT used their judgment to determine sufficient similarities.
Meta-analyses were not excluded solely for statistical heterogeneity (differences across
studies in effect size estimates). Restricted maximum likelihood (REML) model meta-
analyses of the risk ratio for outcomes in Stata were conducted. Where pertinent, the ERT
D
combined existing systematic reviews with additional primary studies using the same
approach as de novo meta-analysis, using data from all primary studies.
Assessment of heterogeneity – Heterogeneity was assessed by visual inspection of
forest plots of standardized mean effect sizes and of RRs, and χ2 tests. A P < 0.05 was used
to denote statistical heterogeneity, with an I2 calculated to measure the proportion of total
variation in the estimates of treatment effect that was due to heterogeneity beyond chance.
Grading the certainty of the evidence
The certainty of the evidence related to each critical and important outcome was
assessed based on a GRADE approach (Table 64),1274 which assesses the certainty of the
evidence for each outcome, with some modifications. For each outcome, the initial grade
for the certainty of evidence started at “high” but was then lowered if there were serious
methodological limitations (i.e., risk of bias) across the aggregate of studies, if there were
clinically important inconsistencies in the results across studies or large statistically
heterogeneity by meta-analysis (unless the direction of effect was consistent), if there was
371

uncertainty about the directness of evidence (including limited applicability of the findings
to the population of interest), if the outcome measure estimates were imprecise or based on
sparse studies, or if there was thought to be a high likelihood of reporting bias. The final
grade for the certainty of the evidence for an outcome could be high (A), moderate (B), low
(C), or very low (D) (Table 65). If there was a large degree of unexplained inconsistency
across studies, very imprecise summary estimates of effect, or only a single small study, the
certainty of evidence for the given outcome was rated very low.
T
In determining the overall certainty of evidence for each comparison of
interventions (or diagnostic test), the primary purpose of the intervention was
prioritized when assessing across prioritized outcomes. Thus, for interventions to
predict or prevent AKI, the overall certainty oFf evidence was determined first based
on the certainty of evidence for incident AKI. However, if the certainty of evidence
for mortality or other prioritized AKI-related outcomes was greater than for incident
AKI, the overall certainty of evidence was graded per the other prioritized outcomes.
A
Similarly for interventions primarily used to manage AKI, the overall certainty of
evidence was first determined based on RRT but was upgraded if the certainty of
evidence for death or MAKE were higher than for RRT.
Evidence profileRs
Evidence profiles were developed to include a description of the population and the
intervention and comparator. In addition, the evidence profiles include risk of bias rating
and results from the data synthesis. The grading of the certainty of the evidence for each
critical and important outcome is also provided in these tables (Data Supplement
D
Appendixes C and D).
372

TabDle 65 | Classification for certainty of evidence
Grade Certainty Meaning
of evidence
A High We are confident that the true effect is close to the estimate of the
effect.
B Moderate The true effect is likely to be close to the estimate of the effect, but
there is a possibility that it is substantially different.
C Low The true effect may be substantially different from the estimate of
the effect.
D Very low The estimate of effect is very uncertain, and often it will be far from
the true effect.
Developing the recommendations
Recommendations from the KDIGO 2012 Clinical Practice Guideline for Acute
Kidney Injury were considered in the context of new evidence and current standards of care
by the Work Group Co-Chairs and Work Group members and updated as appropriate.608
Practice points were not yet proposed as a separate category in 2012, so the KDIGO 2026
Work Group considered the following options: (i) where new evidence did not suggest a
373

change to graded recommendations, the statements were retained as graded
recommendations; (ii) graded recommendations were updated, where appropriate, based on
new evidence; (iii) existing recommendations that fit the criteria for practice points were
rewritten as practice points; and (iv) new guideline statements (both recommendations and
practice points) were generated for new clinical questions in the 2026 update.
Grading the strength of the recommendations
The strength of a recommendation was classified bTy the Work Group as Level 1,
“We recommend” or Level 2, “We suggest” (Table 66). The strength of a recommendation
was determined by the balance of benefits and harms across all critical and important
outcomes, the grading of the overall certainty of the evidence, patient values and
preferences, resource use and costs, and consFiderations for implementation (Table 67).

374

Table 67 | Determinants of the strength of recommendation
Factors Comment
Balance of benefits and The larger the difference between the desirable and undesirable effects, the
harms more likely a Level 1 recommendation is provided. The narrower the
gradient, the more likely a Level 2 recommendation is provided.
Certainty of evidence The higher the certainty of the evidence, the more likely a Level 1
recommendation is warranted. However, there are exceptions for which low
or very low certainty of the evidence will warrant a Level 1 recommendation.
Values and preferences The more variability in values and prefTerences, or the more uncertainty in
values and preferences, the more likely a Level 1 recommendation is
warranted. Values and preferences were obtained from the literature, when
possible, or were assessed by the judgment of the Work Group when robust
evidence was not identified.
F
Resource use and costs The higher the cost of an intervention—that is, the more resources
consumed—the less likely a Level 1 recommendation is warranted.
Balance of benefits and harms - The Work Group determined the anticipated net
A
health benefit on the basis of expected benefits and harms across all critical and important
outcomes from the underlying evidence review.
Overall certainty of evidence - The overall certainty of the evidence for each
recommendationR was determined by the certainty of evidence for all critical and important
outcomes, considering the relative importance of each outcome to the population of
interest, as described above. For recommendations about diagnostic test studies, where
there was evidence only for the diagnostic accuracy of the tests (e.g., to predict AKI) and
no evidence from RCTs that implemented the diagnostic tests, the overall certainty of the
D
evidence to support the recommendation was downgraded 1 level due to indirectness of the
evidence. In addition, when eligible studies included only adults for a given specific topic,
the pediatricians in the Work Group, together with the Co-Chairs and Methods Committee
representatives, determined whether there was concern about applicability to children and,
where there was, the overall certainty of the evidence to support the recommendation was
also downgraded one level due to indirectness. In these instances, the downgrading is
explained in each certainty of evidence description. The final grade for the certainty of the
evidence for an outcome could be high (A), moderate (B), low (C), or very low (D) (Tables
64 and 65).
Patient values and preferences - The Work Group included 2 people with lived
experience of AKI. These members’ unique perspectives and lived experience, in addition
to the Work Group understanding of patient preferences and priorities, informed decisions
about the strength of the recommendations. A systematic review of qualitative studies on
patient priorities and preferences was not undertaken for this guideline.
375

Resource use and costs - Healthcare and non-healthcare resources, including all
inputs in the treatment management pathway, were considered in grading the strength of a
recommendation.468 The following resources were considered: direct healthcare costs, non–
healthcare resources (such as transportation and social services), informal caregiver
resources (e.g., time of family and caregivers), and changes in productivity. No formal
economic evaluations, including cost-effectiveness analysis, were conducted.
Practice points T
In addition to graded recommendations, KDIGO guidelines now include practice
points to help healthcare professionals better evaluate and implement the guidance from the
expert Work Group. Practice points are consensus statements about a specific aspect of
care and supplement recommendations. AlthoFugh systematic reviews are not performed for
clinical questions underlying practice points, they are often crafted to help readers
implement the guidance from graded recommendations. Practice points represent the expert
judgment of the guideline Work Group, and they may be based on limited evidence.
A
Practice points are sometimes formatted as a table, a figure, or an algorithm, to make them
easier to use in clinical practice.
Format for guideline recommendations and practice points
Each guiRdeline recommendation provides an assessment of the strength of the
recommendation (Level 1, “we recommend” or Level 2, “we suggest”) and the overall
certainty of the evidence (high [A], moderate [B], low [C], very low [D]). The
recommendation statements are followed by Key information (Balance of benefits and
harms, Certainty of the evidence, Values and preferences, Resource use and costs,
D
Considerations for implementation) and Rationale. Each recommendation is linked to a
relevant evidence profile. An underlying rationale may also support a practice point.
Limitations of the guideline development process
Although the literature searches were intended to be comprehensive, they were not
exhaustive. MEDLINE and Embase databases were searched, but other specialty or
regional databases were not. Hand searches of journals were not performed, and review
articles and textbook chapters were not systematically searched; however, existing
systematic review and clinical practice guidelines were hand-searched. In addition, any
important studies known to domain experts that were missed by the electronic literature
searches were added to retrieved articles and reviewed by the Work Group for eligibility.
The ERT did not systematically review all topics of interventions that are amenable
to trials. Topics were prioritized by the Work Group. Other topics were addressed by
practice points. Almost all research questions pertaining to interventions were restricted to
RCTs. Other study designs that may inform the guideline were not systematically
376

reviewed. We did not review qualitative research studies (e.g., focus groups) to inform
determinations about values and preferences, or cost-effectiveness analyses to inform
determinations about resource use or costs.
T
F
A
R
D
